line=3813 tag=L:           (3)      a.       Kada        a     yi haka
line=3814 tag=B:
line=3815 tag=G:                             shouldn't Ind. do thus
line=3816 tag=B:
line=3817 tag=B:
line=3818 tag=M:  24
line=3819 tag=M:            That generic nouns may be regarded as identifiable can be proved by the fact that they may be
line=3820 tag=M:  anaphorically referred to by a definite pronoun. For example, A book is a useful thing to have in a doctor's
line=3821 tag=M:  waiting room. It is also something easy to carry around (Lambrecht 1994:82).
line=3822 tag=M:            Other studies divide the generic into `definite generic' and `indefinite generic' (e.g. Carlson and
line=3823 tag=M:  Pelletier ed. 1995).
line=3824 tag=B:
line=3825 tag=M:                                                                                                  79
line=3826 tag=T:                         `You/one shouldn't do that.'

line=133 tag=B:
line=134 tag=M:  the traditional term "relative". I chose the terms "focus -perfective" and "focus-i mperfective"
line=135 tag=M:  in order to provide a unified semantic characterization of (most of) the syntactic
line=136 tag=M:  environments which require these special TAMs. Alth ough these reductionist labels are not
line=137 tag=M:  ideal, I still think that on balance they are preferable to the narrow and potentially misleading
line=138 tag=M:  terms "relative-p erfective" and "relative-imp erfective" (see in particular the discussion on
line=139 tag=M:  pp. 162-4). Th e focus INFL is also used in relative claus es (hence the conventional term), and
line=140 tag=M:  these overlap considerably with subordinate interrog ative constructions, cf. (subordinate
line=141 tag=M:  interrogative) naa sa n a~bîn da~ yake~e neemaa `I kno w what he's looking for' (... thing.the
line=142 tag=M:  REL 3MS.FOC-IMPFV looking for), and (relative construction) ga~a a~bîn da~ ya ke~e neemaa
line=143 tag=M:  `here is what he's looking for'.
line=144 tag=M:    The focus-mark er/copula (also used in equational and identificational constructions) is also
line=145 tag=B:  4
line=146 tag=B:
line=147 tag=M:  present, in segmentally rep eated form, in the fully expanded variants of the personal wa~a
line=148 tag=M:  `who?' and non-personal me~e `wh at?' WH-pronouns, i.e., (MS) wa~a -nee-ne~e `who (is it)?',
line=149 tag=M:  me~e-nee-ne~e `what (is it)?' (see exx. 24a, 25a).

language: Hausa (hau)
line=385 tag=L+EX:        b.   yana~a í~naa, Îaa ta~n?       (follow-up with in-situ locative WH-adjunct)
line=386 tag=G:             3MS.IMPFV where eggplant.the
line=387 tag=B:
line=388 tag=B:  9
line=389 tag=M:     When we move to embedded subordinate clauses, ho wev er, my impression is that it is not
line=390 tag=M:  possible for the interrogative locative phrase to remain in situ in such nonverbal tensed
line=391 tag=M:  constructions, i.e., *sai ta ta~mba~yi sa ní~n [suna~a í~na a] `then she asked where they were'
line=392 tag=M:  (then 3FS.PFV ask kno wing.of 3PL.IMPFV where) is ruled out, and only sai ta ta~mba~yi saní~n
line=393 tag=M:  [inda~ suke~] (... kno wing.of wh ere 3PL.FOC-IMPFV) is admissable, where initial position inda ~
line=394 tag=M:  `wh ere' is the complex relative form co rresponding to interrogative í~naa.
line=395 tag=B:  10
line=396 tag=M:      In Standard Hausa, clauses containing a WH-element usually attach a Lo w tone
line=397 tag=M:  interrogative morpheme with vowel length /`:/ to the final constituent (the so-called "q-
line=398 tag=M:  morpheme), and I am assuming this applies whether the WH-element is ex situ or in situ. If
line=399 tag=M:  the word h as a final High tone, as in (17a), the output is a Falling tone on a long vo wel, i.e.,
line=400 tag=M:  suna~a í~naa ya~nzu + /`:/  [suna~a í~naa ya~nzu$u]. See Newman & Newman (1981) for
line=401 tag=M:  details.
line=402 tag=B:
line=403 tag=B:                                                                                                   9
line=404 tag=M:  Jaggar: Hausa wh /focus
line=405 tag=B:
line=406 tag=T+DB+CR+LT:             `wh ere is it, the eggplant?' (lit. `it is where, the eggplant?')
